id="mw-page-base"></div>\n<div class="noprint" id="mw-head-base"></div>\n<div class="mw-body" id="content" role="main">\n<a id="top"></a>\n<div class="mw-body-content" id="siteNotice"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Comparison of Asian national space programs</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Asia%27s_Space_Race&amp;redirect=no" title="Asia\'s Space Race">Asia\'s Space Race</a>)</span></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p>Several Asian countries have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_space_agencies" title="List of space agencies">space programs</a> and are actively competing to achieve scientific and technological advancements in <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a>, a situation sometimes referred to as the <b>Asian space race</b> in the popular media<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> as a reference to the earlier <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> between the United States and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. Like the previous space race, issues involved in the current push to space include <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">national security</a>, which has spurred many countries to send artificial <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">humans</a> into <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_orbit" title="Geocentric orbit">Earth orbit</a> and beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-2">[2]</a></sup> A number of Asian countries are seen as contenders in the ongoing race to be the pre-eminent power in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Asian_space_powers"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Japan</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Other_minor_players"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Bangladesh"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span class="toctext">Bangladesh</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Other_nations_and_regions"><span class="tocnumber">2.7</span> <span class="toctext">Other nations and regions</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Orbital_Launch_Frequency"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Orbital Launch Frequency</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#2001-2010"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">2001-2010</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#2011-2018"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">2011-2018</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Solar_System_exploration"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Solar System exploration</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Moon_race"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Moon race</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-21"><a href="#Probing_the_Moon"><span class="tocnumber">6.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Probing the Moon</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-22"><a href="#Moon_landings"><span class="tocnumber">6.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Moon landings</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Exploration_of_the_major_planets"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Exploration of the major planets</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Asian_space_agencies_and_programs"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Asian space agencies and programs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Notes_and_references"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes and references</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asian_space_powers">Asian space powers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Asian space powers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_first_orbital_launches_by_country" title="Timeline of first orbital launches by country">Timeline of first orbital launches by country</a></div>\n<p>Of the ten countries that have independently <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_first_orbital_launches_by_country" title="Timeline of first orbital launches by country">successfully launched a satellite into orbit</a>, six are Asian: China, India, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, Israel, Japan and <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>.\n</p><p>China\'s first manned spacecraft entered orbit in October 2003, making China the first Asian nation to <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">send a human</a> into space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-4">[4]</a></sup> India expects to send astronauts to space in the <a href="/wiki/Gaganyaan" title="Gaganyaan">Gaganyaan</a> capsule by 2022.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the achievements of space programs run by the main Asian space players (China, India, and Japan) pale in comparison to the milestones set by the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and the United States, some experts believe Asia may soon lead the world in space exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> China has been the leader of Asia\'s space race since the beginning of the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The first Chinese manned spaceflight, in 2003, marked the beginning of a space race in the region. At the same time, the existence of a space race in Asia is still debated due to the non-concurrence of space milestone events like there was for the United States and the Soviet Union. Japan for example was the first power on Earth to get a sample return mission from an asteroid. There was however some concurrence between China and India to see which of those two could be the first to launch a probe to the Earth\'s moon back in the late 2000s decade. China, for example, denies that there is an Asian space race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>\nIn January 2007 China became the first Asian military-space power to send an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-satellite" title="Anti-satellite">anti-satellite</a> missile into orbit, to destroy an aging Chinese Feng Yun 1C <a href="/wiki/Weather_satellite" title="Weather satellite">weather satellite</a> in polar orbit. The resulting explosion sent a wave of debris hurtling through space at more than 6&#160;miles per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> A month later, <a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">Japan\'s space agency</a> launched an experimental communications satellite designed to enable super high-speed data transmission in remote areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After successful achievement of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geostationary" title="Geostationary">geostationary</a> technology, India\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISRO" title="ISRO">ISRO</a> launched its first Moon mission, <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-1" title="Chandrayaan-1">Chandrayaan-1</a> in October 2008, which discovered <a href="/wiki/Lunar_water" title="Lunar water">ice water on the Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> India then launched on 5 November 2013 its maiden interplanetary mission, the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Mission" title="Mars Orbiter Mission">Mars Orbiter Mission</a>. The primary objective is to determine Mars\' atmospheric composition and attempt to detect methane. The spacecraft completed its journey on 24 September 2014 when it entered its intended orbit around Mars, making India the first Asian country to successfully place a Mars orbiter and the only country in history to do so in the first attempt. ISRO became the fourth space agency in the world to send a spacecraft to Mars, only behind NASA, ROSCOSMOS, and ESA.\n</p><p>In addition to increasing national pride, countries are commercially motivated to operate in space. Commercial satellites are launched for communications, weather forecasting, and atmospheric research. According to a report by the <a href="/wiki/Space_Frontier_Foundation" title="Space Frontier Foundation">Space Frontier Foundation</a> released in 2006, the "space economy" is estimated to be worth about $180&#160;billion, with more than 60% of space-related economic activity coming from commercial goods and services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-2">[2]</a></sup> China and India propose the initiation of a commercial launch service.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_space_program" title="Chinese space program">Chinese space program</a></div>\n<p>China has a space program with an independent <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">human spaceflight</a> capability. It has developed a sizable family of successful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_March_rockets" title="Long March rockets">Long March rockets</a>. It has launched two lunar orbiters, <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_1" title="Chang\'e 1">Chang\'e 1</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_2" title="Chang\'e 2">Chang\'e 2</a>. On 2 December 2013, China launched a modified Long March 3B rocket, with <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_3" title="Chang\'e 3">Chang\'e 3</a> Moon lander and its rover Yutu on-board toward the Moon and successfully performed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_landing_(rocketry)" title="Soft landing (rocketry)">soft landing</a> and rover operations, becoming the third country to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> It also has plans to retrieve samples by late 2017. In 2011, China embarked on a program to establish a manned <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a>, starting with the launch of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiangong_1" title="Tiangong 1">Tiangong 1</a> and followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiangong_2" title="Tiangong 2">Tiangong 2</a> in 2016. China attempted to send a Mars orbiter (<a href="/wiki/Yinghuo-1" title="Yinghuo-1">Yinghuo-1</a>) in 2011 on a joint mission with Russia, which failed to leave Earth orbit. Nevertheless, the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Remote_Sensing_Orbiter_and_Small_Rover" title="Mars Global Remote Sensing Orbiter and Small Rover">2020 Chinese Mars Mission</a> with an orbiter, a lander and a rover has been approved by the government and is aiming a launch date in the year 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-13">[13]</a></sup> China has collaborative projects with Russia, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">ESA</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, and has launched commercial satellites for other countries. Some analysts suggest that the Chinese space program is linked to the nation\'s efforts at developing advanced military technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>China\'s advanced technology is the result of the integration of various related technological experiences. Early Chinese satellites, such as the <a href="/wiki/Fanhui_Shi_Weixing" title="Fanhui Shi Weixing">FSW</a> series, have undergone many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_reentry" title="Atmospheric reentry">atmospheric reentry</a> tests. In the 1990s China had commercial launches, resulting in more launch experiences and a high success rate after the 1990s. China has aimed to undertake scientific development in fields like Solar System exploration. China\'s Shenzhou 7 spacecraft successfully performed an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">EVA</a> in September 2008. China\'s Shenzhou 9 spacecraft successfully performed a manned docking in June 2012. Furthermore, China\'s Chang\'e 2 explorer became the first object to reach Sun-Earth Lagrangian point in August 2011 and also the first probe to explore both Moon and asteroid by making a flyby of the asteroid <a href="/wiki/4179_Toutatis" title="4179 Toutatis">4179 Toutatis</a>. China has launched <a href="/wiki/Dark_Matter_Particle_Explorer" title="Dark Matter Particle Explorer">DAMPE</a>, the most capable dark matter explorer to date in 2015, and world\'s first quantum communication satellite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/QUESS" title="QUESS">QUESS</a> in 2016.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">Indian Space Research Organisation</a></div>\n<p>India\'s interest in space travel began in the early 1960s, when scientists launched a <a href="/wiki/Nike-Apache" title="Nike-Apache">Nike-Apache</a> rocket from <a href="/wiki/Thumba_Equatorial_Rocket_Launching_Station" title="Thumba Equatorial Rocket Launching Station">TERLS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Under <a href="/wiki/Vikram_Sarabhai" title="Vikram Sarabhai">Vikram Sarabhai</a>, the program focused on the practical uses of space in increasing the standard of living. Remote sensing and communications satellites were placed into orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first Indian to travel in space was <a href="/wiki/Rakesh_Sharma" title="Rakesh Sharma">Rakesh Sharma</a>, who flew aboard <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_T-11" title="Soyuz T-11">Soyuz T-11</a>, launched April 2, 1984, from erstwhile <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">USSR</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Just a few days after China said that it would send a human into orbit in the second half of 2003, Indian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Atal_Bihari_Vajpayee" title="Atal Bihari Vajpayee">Atal Bihari Vajpayee</a> publicly urged his country\'s scientists to work towards sending a man to the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> It successfully sent its probe to the Moon in October 2008<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and is planning its second Moon mission, <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-2" title="Chandrayaan-2">Chandrayaan-2</a> for 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISRO" title="ISRO">ISRO</a> launched its <a href="/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Mission" title="Mars Orbiter Mission">Mars Orbiter Mission</a> on November 5, 2013 (informally called "Mangalyaan") which successfully entered into the orbit around Mars on 24 September 2014. India is the first in Asia and fourth in the world to perform a successful Mars mission. It is also the only one to do so on the first attempt and at a record low cost of $74 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISRO" title="ISRO">ISRO</a> has demonstrated its re-entry technology and till date has launched as many as 175 foreign satellites belonging to global customers from 20 countries including US, Germany, France, Japan, Canada, U.K. All of these have been launched successfully by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PSLV" title="PSLV">PSLVs</a> so far, gaining significant expertise in space technologies. In June 2016, India set a record by launching 20 satellites simultaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20_Satellite_launch_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-20_Satellite_launch-24">[24]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PSLV" title="PSLV">PSLVs</a> are also one of world\'s most reliable launch vehicles which clocked its 35th successful mission (39 total) in a row as of February 2017, thus having success rate of nearly 90%.\n</p><p>India broke the world record by successfully placing 104 satellites (almost tripling the Russian record of 37) in Earth Orbit on 15 February 2017 on a single rocket launch (<a href="/wiki/PSLV-C37" title="PSLV-C37">PSLV-C37</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time.com_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-time.com-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Recent reports indicate that human spaceflight is planned with a spacecraft called <i><a href="/wiki/Gaganyaan" title="Gaganyaan">Gaganyaan</a></i> for December 2021 on a home-grown <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GSLV_Mk_III" title="GSLV Mk III">GSLV-III rocket</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>ISRO is also planning to send orbiters to Venus, Mars and Jupiter or comets and asteroids in the near future.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">JAXA</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H2A11001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/H2A11001.jpg/220px-H2A11001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/H2A11001.jpg/330px-H2A11001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/H2A11001.jpg/440px-H2A11001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H2A11001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/H-IIA" title="H-IIA">H-IIA</a> F11 launch vehicle lifts off from <a href="/wiki/Tanegashima_Space_Center" title="Tanegashima Space Center">Tanegashima Space Center</a> in Japan</div></div></div>\n<p>Japan has been cooperating with the United States on missile defence since 1999. <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korean</a> nuclear and Chinese military programs represent a serious issue for Japan\'s foreign relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\nJapan is working on military and civilian space technologies, developing missile defence systems, new generations of military spy satellites, and planning for manned stations on the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\nJapan started to construct spy satellites after North Korea test fired a <a href="/wiki/Taepodong-1" title="Taepodong-1">Taepodong</a> missile over Japan in 1998. The North Korean government claimed the missile was merely launching a satellite to space, and accused Japan of causing an arms race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The Japanese constitution adopted after World War II limits military activities to defensive operations. On May 2007 Prime Minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shinzo_Abe" title="Shinzo Abe">Shinzo Abe</a> called for a bold review of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Constitution" title="Japanese Constitution">Japanese Constitution</a> to allow the country to take a larger role in global security and foster a revival of national pride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Japan has not yet developed its own manned spacecraft and does not have a program in place to develop one. The Japanese space shuttle <a href="/wiki/HOPE-X" title="HOPE-X">HOPE-X</a>, to be launched by the conventional space launcher <a href="/wiki/H-II" title="H-II">H-II</a>, was developed but the program was postponed and eventually cancelled. Then the simpler manned capsule <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuji_(Spacecraft)" title="Fuji (Spacecraft)">Fuji</a> was proposed but not adopted. Pioneer projects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single_stage_to_orbit" title="Single stage to orbit">single-stage to orbit</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reusable_launch_vehicle" title="Reusable launch vehicle">reusable launch vehicle</a> horizontal takeoff and landing ASSTS and <a href="/wiki/VTOL" title="VTOL">vertical takeoff and landing</a> <a href="/wiki/Kankoh-maru" title="Kankoh-maru">Kankoh-maru</a> were developed but have not been adopted. A more conservative new (<a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">JAXA manned spacecraft</a>) project is proposed to launch by 2025 as part of the Japanese plan to send manned missions to the Moon. <a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E6%9D%BE%E6%B5%A6%E6%99%8B%E4%B9%9F" title="ja:&#26494;&#28006;&#26187;&#20063;">Shin\'ya Matsuura</a> is doubtful about the Japanese manned Moon project, and suspects the project is a euphemism for participation in the American <a href="/wiki/Constellation_program" title="Constellation program">Constellation program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nikkeibp_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-nikkeibp-32">[32]</a></sup> JAXA planned to send a <a href="/wiki/Humanoid_robot" title="Humanoid robot">humanoid robot</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/ASIMO" title="ASIMO">ASIMO</a>) to the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nikkeibp_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-nikkeibp-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (October 2014)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_minor_players">Other minor players</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Other minor players">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran">Iran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Iran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Space_Agency" title="Iranian Space Agency">Iranian Space Agency</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Simorgh_SLV.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="820" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Simorgh_SLV.png/100px-Simorgh_SLV.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Simorgh_SLV.png/150px-Simorgh_SLV.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Simorgh_SLV.png/200px-Simorgh_SLV.png 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Simorgh_SLV.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iranian <a href="/wiki/Simorgh_(rocket)" title="Simorgh (rocket)">Simorgh (rocket)</a> SLV</div></div></div>\n<p>Iran has developed its own satellite launch vehicle, named the <a href="/wiki/Safir_(rocket)" title="Safir (rocket)">Safir</a> SLV, based on the <a href="/wiki/Shahab-1" title="Shahab-1">Shahab</a> series of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IRBM" title="IRBM">IRBMs</a>. On 2 February 2009, Iranian state television reported that Iran\'s first domestically made satellite <a href="/wiki/Omid" title="Omid">Omid</a> (from the Persian &#1575;&#1605;&#1740;&#1583;, meaning "Hope") had been successfully launched into <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> by a version of Iran\'s Safir rocket, the Safir-2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The launch coincided with the 30th anniversary of the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">Iranian Revolution</a>. Iran is also developing a new launch vehicle <a href="/wiki/Simorgh_(rocket)" title="Simorgh (rocket)">Simorgh (rocket)</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Israel">Israel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Israel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Israel_Space_Agency" title="Israel Space Agency">Israel Space Agency</a></div>\n<p>Israel became the tenth country in the world to build its own satellite and launch it with its own launcher on 19 September 1988. Israel launched its first satellite, <a href="/wiki/Ofeq" title="Ofeq">Ofeq</a>-1, using an Israeli-built <a href="/wiki/Shavit" title="Shavit">Shavit</a> three-stage launch vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The launching was the high point of a process that began in 1983 with the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Israel_Space_Agency" title="Israel Space Agency">Israel Space Agency</a> under the aegis of the Ministry of Science. Space research by university-based scientists began in the 1960s, providing a ready-made pool of experts for Israel\'s foray into space. Since then, local universities, research institutes, and private industry, backed by the Israel Space Agency, have made progress in space technology. The agency\'s role is to support "private and academic space projects, coordinate their efforts, initiate and develop international relations and projects, head integrative projects involving different bodies, and create public awareness for the importance of space development."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Korea">North Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: North Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_Aerospace_Development_Administration" title="National Aerospace Development Administration">National Aerospace Development Administration</a></div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DPRK" title="DPRK">North Korea</a> has many years of experience with rocket technology, which it has passed along to Pakistan and other countries. On December 12, 2012, North Korea placed its first satellite in orbit with the launch of <a href="/wiki/Kwangmy%C5%8Fngs%C5%8Fng-3_Unit_2" title="Kwangmy&#335;ngs&#335;ng-3 Unit 2">Kwangmy&#335;ngs&#335;ng-3 Unit 2</a>. On 12 March 2009 North Korea signed the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Space_Treaty" title="Outer Space Treaty">Outer Space Treaty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Registration_Convention" title="Registration Convention">Registration Convention</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> after a previous declaration of preparations for the launch of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kwangmyongsong-2" title="Kwangmyongsong-2">Kwangmyongsong-2</a>. North Korea twice announced satellite launches: <a href="/wiki/Kwangmy%C5%8Fngs%C5%8Fng-1" title="Kwangmy&#335;ngs&#335;ng-1">Kwangmy&#335;ngs&#335;ng-1</a> on 31 August 1998 and Kwangmy&#335;ngs&#335;ng-2 on 5 April 2009. Neither of these claims were confirmed by the rest of the world, but the United States and South Korea believe there were tests of military ballistic missiles. The North Korean space agency is the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Committee_of_Space_Technology" title="Korean Committee of Space Technology">Korean Committee of Space Technology</a>, which operates the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musudan-ri" title="Musudan-ri">Musudan-ri</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tongch%27ang-dong_Space_Launch_Center" title="Tongch\'ang-dong Space Launch Center">Tongch\'ang-dong Space Launch Center</a> rocket launching sites and has developed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paektusan_(rocket)" title="Paektusan (rocket)">Baekdusan-1</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unha" title="Unha">Unha (Baekdusan-2)</a> space launchers and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kwangmy%C5%8Fngs%C5%8Fng_programme" title="Kwangmy&#335;ngs&#335;ng programme">Kwangmy&#335;ngs&#335;ng</a> satellites. In 2009 North Korea announced several future space projects, including manned space flights and the development of a manned partially reusable launch vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The successor to the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Committee_of_Space_Technology" title="Korean Committee of Space Technology">Korean Committee of Space Technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Aerospace_Development_Administration" title="National Aerospace Development Administration">National Aerospace Development Administration</a> (NADA) successfully launched an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unha-3" title="Unha-3">Unha-3</a> <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicle</a> in February of 2016, placing the <a href="/wiki/Kwangmy%C5%8Fngs%C5%8Fng-4" title="Kwangmy&#335;ngs&#335;ng-4">Kwangmy&#335;ngs&#335;ng-4</a> satellite in orbit.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Korea">South Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: South Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Korea_Aerospace_Research_Institute" title="Korea Aerospace Research Institute">Korea Aerospace Research Institute</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> is a newer player in the Asian space race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In August 2006 South Korea launched its first military communications satellite, the Mugunghwa-5. The satellite was placed in <a href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_orbit" title="Geosynchronous orbit">geosynchronous orbit</a> and collects surveillance information about North Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The South Korean government is spending hundreds of millions of dollars in space technology and was due to launch its first space launcher, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korea_Space_Launch_Vehicle" title="Korea Space Launch Vehicle">Korea Space Launch Vehicle</a>, in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (October 2014)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> South Korea\'s government justifies the cost for reasons of long-term commercial benefits and national pride. South Korea has long seen North Korea\'s significantly longer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_range" title="Rocket range">missile range</a> as a serious threat to its national security. With the nation\'s first astronaut launched into space, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yi_So-Yeon" title="Yi So-Yeon">Lee So-yeon</a>, South Korea gained confidence in entering the Asian space race. They have completed the construction of <a href="/wiki/Naro_Space_Center" title="Naro Space Center">Naro Space Center</a>. South Korea is now attempting to build satellites and rockets with local technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> South Korea is pursuing a space program that could defend the peninsula while lessening their dependency on the United States.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Pakistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_and_Upper_Atmosphere_Research_Commission" title="Space and Upper Atmosphere Research Commission">Space and Upper Atmosphere Research Commission</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> started pursuing space technology on 16 September 1961, when Pakistan\'s space agency, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SUPARCO" title="SUPARCO">SUPARCO</a> was created, with renowned physicist <a href="/wiki/Abdus_Salam" title="Abdus Salam">Abdus Salam</a> as its first administrator. In its early days, SUPARCO researched on the development of solid-fuel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sounding_rockets" title="Sounding rockets">sounding rockets</a> with assistance provided by the United States. On 7 June 1962, with the launch of the <i><a href="/wiki/Rehbar-I" title="Rehbar-I">Rehbar-I</a></i> (<i>lit. Teller of the Way</i>) rocket, Pakistan became the tenth country in the world to successfully conduct the launch of an unmanned spacecraft. This rocket had been developed by a team of scientists and engineers of the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Air_Force" title="Pakistan Air Force">Pakistan Air Force</a>, led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_Commodore" title="Air Commodore">Air Commodore</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brigadier-General" title="Brigadier-General">Brigadier-General</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W%C5%82adys%C5%82aw_J%C3%B3zef_Marian_Turowicz" title="W&#322;adys&#322;aw J&#243;zef Marian Turowicz">W&#322;adys&#322;aw J&#243;zef Marian Turowicz</a> in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> and was launched from <a href="/wiki/Sonmiani" title="Sonmiani">Sonmiani</a>, Pakistan\'s first space launch facility. SUPARCO\'s unmanned space program continued till 1972, with nearly 20 successful launches. SUPARCO\'s unmanned space program suffered setbacks during the 1970s and the 1980s, delaying the development and launch of Pakistan\'s first satellite, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Badr-I" title="Badr-I">Badr-I</a> till 1990 when it was launched from China. SUPARCO launched Pakistan\'s second satellite, <a href="/wiki/Badr-B" title="Badr-B">Badr-B</a> in 2001 from <a href="/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome" title="Baikonur Cosmodrome">Baikonur Cosmodrome</a> using a Ukrainian <a href="/wiki/Zenit-2" title="Zenit-2">Zenit-2</a> rocket, followed by <a href="/wiki/Paksat-1R" title="Paksat-1R">Paksat-1R</a> in 2011 which was contracted and actually built and launched by China, was Pakistan\'s first communication satellite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> On 9 July, 2018, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> launched two satellites from <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jiuquan_Satellite_Launch_Centre" title="Jiuquan Satellite Launch Centre">Jiuquan Satellite Launch Centre</a> with <a href="/wiki/Long_March_2C" title="Long March 2C">Long March 2C Rocket</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Remote_Sensing_Satellite" title="Pakistan Remote Sensing Satellite">Pakistan Remote Sensing Satellite (PRSS-1)</a> was a dual-purpose Earth observational and optical satellite. It was designed and developed by SUPARCO&#8217;s engineers. It was 1,200 kilograms (2,600&#160;lb) weight satellite which will operate at an altitude of 640 kilometres (400&#160;mi). With the launching of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Remote_Sensing_Satellite" title="Pakistan Remote Sensing Satellite">PRSS-1</a> , <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> has become one of the few countries to have its own remote sensing satellite in the orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Express_Tribune_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Express_Tribune-44">[44]</a></sup> Another flagship satellite, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Technology_Evaluation_Satellite" title="Pakistan Technology Evaluation Satellite">Pakistan Technology Evaluation Satellite (PakTES-1A)</a> which was also designed and developed by SUPARCO&#8217;s engineers, has also co-launched with <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Remote_Sensing_Satellite" title="Pakistan Remote Sensing Satellite">PRSS-1</a> by the same launch rocket, <a href="/wiki/Long_March_2C" title="Long March 2C">Long March 2C</a>. It was a 285 kilograms (628&#160;lb) weight satellite with an optical payload commensurate with national needs. It will operate at an altitude of 610 kilometres (380&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Express_Tribune_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Express_Tribune-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> intends to send its first national in space onboard a Chinese spacecraft by 2022, as announced by information minister <a href="/wiki/Fawad_Chaudhry" title="Fawad Chaudhry">Chaudhry</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bangladesh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Research_and_Remote_Sensing_Organization" title="Space Research and Remote Sensing Organization">Space Research and Remote Sensing Organization</a></div>\n<p>With the planned launched of <a href="/wiki/Bangabandhu-1" title="Bangabandhu-1">Bangabandhu-1</a> satellite purchased abroad, Bangladesh will operate its first communication satellite. Bangladesh Space Agency intends to launch satellites after 2020. Bangladesh\'s government has stressed that the country seeks an "entirely peaceful and commercial" role in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_nations_and_regions">Other nations and regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Other nations and regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> operates their own communication satellites purchased abroad, and intends to develop and use their own small space launch vehicle <a href="/wiki/RPS-420" title="RPS-420">Pengorbitan (RPS-420)</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (October 2014)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Other space players are <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, that announced multi-task space programs in 2006 and 2007.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> They intend to develop their own satellites and launchers in the near future, and manned space facilities. As of 2012 Turkey was developing its own <a href="/wiki/Military_satellite" title="Military satellite">military satellite</a>. The first <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Air_Force#Satellites" title="Turkish Air Force">G&#246;kt&#252;rk satellite</a> is planned to be launched in 2013.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (October 2014)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> The Turkish satellite is planned to be capable of taking satellite images of greater than two meters per pixel resolution, thus making Turkey the second nation in the world capable of such a feat, after the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" class="mw-headline" id="Solar_System_exploration">Solar System exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Solar System exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Solar_System_exploration" title="Timeline of Solar System exploration">Solar System exploration</a> and manned spaceflights are major space technologies in the public eye. Since <a href="/wiki/Sakigake" title="Sakigake">Sakigake</a>, the first interplanetary probe in Asia, was launched in 1985, Japan has completed the most planetary exploration, but other nations are catching up.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moon_race">Moon race</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Moon race">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Moon is thought to be rich in <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">Helium-3</a>, which could one day be used in <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">nuclear fusion power plants</a> to fuel future <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_energy_resources_and_consumption" title="World energy resources and consumption">energy demands</a> in Asia. All three main Asian space powers plan to send men to the Moon in the distant future and have already sent <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft#Lunar_probes" title="Robotic spacecraft">lunar probes</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Probing_the_Moon">Probing the Moon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Probing the Moon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Japan was the first Asian country to launch a lunar probe. The <a href="/wiki/Hiten" title="Hiten">Hiten</a> (Japanese: "flying angel") spacecraft (known before the launch as MUSES-A), built by the Institute of Space and Astronautical Science of Japan, was launched on 24 January 1990. In many ways, the mission did not go as was planned. <a href="/wiki/SELENE" title="SELENE">Kaguya</a>, the second Japanese lunar orbiter spacecraft, was launched on 14 September 2007.\n</p><p>China launched its first lunar probe, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chang%27e-1" title="Chang\'e-1">Chang\'e-1</a>, on 24 October 2007 and successfully entered lunar orbit on 5 November 2007.\n</p><p>India launched its first lunar probe, <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-1" title="Chandrayaan-1">Chandrayaan-1</a>, on 22 October 2008 and successfully entered its final lunar orbit on 2 November 2008. The mission was considered a major success and the probe detected water on the lunar surface.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Moon_landings">Moon landings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Moon landings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">Moon landing</a></div>\n<p>The first confirmed Moon landing from Asia was Hiten\'s mission in 1993. An intentional hard landing at the end of the mission, some pictures of the lunar surface were taken before impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Hiten was not designed as a Moon lander and had few scientific instruments for lunar exploration. The next Japanese Moon landing program was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LUNAR-A" title="LUNAR-A">LUNAR-A</a>, developed from 1992. Although the LUNAR-A orbiter was cancelled, its penetrators are integrated into the Russian <a href="/wiki/Luna-Glob" title="Luna-Glob">Luna-Glob</a> program, which was scheduled to launch in 2011. The penetrators are "relatively" hard landers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> but they are not expected to be destroyed at impact.\n</p><p>The first Asian probe that was part of a lunar landing program was the Indian <a href="/wiki/Moon_Impact_Probe" title="Moon Impact Probe">Moon Impact Probe</a> (MIP) released from <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-1" title="Chandrayaan-1">Chandrayaan-1</a> in 2008. MIP was a hard lander and was designed to move the ground under for research purposes. MIP was designed to be destroyed at impact. Its instruments performed lunar observations to within 25 minutes before impact. The landing test will be applied to future soft landings such as <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-2" title="Chandrayaan-2">Chandrayaan-2</a>, planned for 2019.\n</p><p>The Chinese Chang\'e-1 spacecraft also achieved a systematic hard landing at the end of its mission in 2009, when China became the sixth country to reach the lunar surface. One purpose of the lander was to pre-test for future soft landings. A Chinese lunar soft lander is achieved with the Chang\'e-3 mission.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration_of_the_major_planets">Exploration of the major planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comparison_of_Asian_national_space_programs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Exploration of the major planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Japanese interplanetary probes have been mostly limited to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_bodies" title="Small Solar System bodies">Small Solar System bodies</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">comets</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroids" title="Asteroids">asteroids</a>. JAXA\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nozomi_(probe)" title="Nozomi (probe)">Nozomi probe</a> was launched in 1998, but contact was lost with the probe due to electrical failures before visiting the planet Mars. The second Japanese probe for the planet Venus, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akatsuki_(probe)" title="Akatsuki (probe)">Akatsuki</a>, was launched in 2010. Akatsuki entered orbit around Venus on December 7, 2015.\n</p><p>Chinese scientists expect that China will take 20&#160;years to launch independent planetary probes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The Chinese manned Mars exploration program is planned for around 2050 by the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Chinese Academy of Sciences">Chinese Academy of Sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>\n</p><p>India has successfully launched <a href="/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Mission" title="Mars Orbiter Mission">Mars Orbiter Mission</a> on November 5, 2013. It reached Mars on September 2014. India has become the only country to successfully insert a satellite into Martian orbit in its maiden attempt; it also became the first Asian country to achieve this feat.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asian_space_agencies_and_programs">Asian space agencies and programs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
